<p>Questionnaire must be pre-tested before executing the survey. This helps to identify and eliminate questions that are defective or may lead to wrong information. You may even need to rephrase the questionnaire so that it can elicit the correct responses.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-information">Types of Information<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-information" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Questionnaires can help you collect four different types of information.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="understanding">Understanding<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#understanding" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Information about what people know or how well they understand something, that is, knowledge. For example, what is the major cause of accidental deaths among children in the home?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="beliefs-attitudes-and-opinions">Beliefs, Attitudes and Opinions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#beliefs-attitudes-and-opinions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Information about people's beliefs, attitudes and opinions. Here you would be asking people to share with you their thoughts, feelings, ideas, judgment or their way of thinking. For example, in your opinion does positive self-esteem prevent drug abuse among adolescents?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="behaviour">Behaviour<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#behaviour" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Information about people's behaviour. That is, what people have done in the past, present, and what they plan to do in the future. For example, have you ever attended an antenatal clinic?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="attributes">Attributes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#attributes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Information about peoples attributes. That is, their personal or demographic characteristics.</p>
<p>For example, age, education, occupation and income.</p>
<p>When you design a questionnaire you should be very clear about the objectives and type of information you desire to collect. Otherwise you may end up collecting peoples opinions when in actual fact you wanted to document their behaviour.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="remember">Remember:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#remember" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The response or information you obtain is only as good as the question. To get correct information you must ask the right question.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-question">Types of Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A questionnaire should be laid out in such a way that it provides easy flow from one topic to another. It should have both open- and closed-ended questions. They should be arranged in such a way that they allow natural flow of discussion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="open-ended-question">Open-ended Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#open-ended-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An open-ended question is a type of question that allows the respondent to provide their own answer. It encourages the respondent to think and describe a situation in their own words. The respondent is not given any answers to select from. The answer given is best recorded in the respondent's own words. Although it is the easiest way to ask for information the responses are not easy to analyse. The answers are bound to be varied and so you need to categorise and summarise them.</p>
<p>Open-ended questions are useful because they give more information on:</p>
<ul>
<li>Facts and details which the researcher may not be familiar with</li>
<li>Opinions, attitudes and suggestions</li>
<li>Sensitive issues</li>
</ul>
<p>The following are examples of open-ended questions:</p>
<ol>
<li>What did the traditional birth attendants do when your labour started?</li>
<li>What do you think are the reasons for the high dropout rate of health committee members?</li>
<li>What would you do if you noticed that your daughter (a schoolgirl) has a relationship with her teacher?</li>
</ol>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>As you can see, these questions require deeper thinking and provoke the respondent to elaborate when responding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="closed-ended-question">Closed-ended Question<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#closed-ended-question" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are questions that offer the respondents a list of possible answers to choose from. They are specific and useful when you are interested in certain aspects of an issue. Although they produce more uniform answers than open-ended questions, they depend upon our knowing and including all the relevant answers in the list. To view a table giving three examples of closed-ended questions click the link below.</p>
<p>table of examples of closed ended questions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-1">Example 1<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-2">Example 2<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="example-3">Example 3<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#example-3" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>How useful have the activities of the village health committee been in the development of this village?</p>
<p>Tick the box corresponding with the correct answer.</p>
<ol>
<li>Extremely useful</li>
<li>Very useful</li>
<li>Not very useful</li>
<li>Not useful at all</li>
</ol>
<p>A good questionnaire should cover the following topics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Measurement of the community's health status</li>
<li>Anthropometric measurements</li>
<li>Physical examination</li>
<li>Laboratory tests</li>
</ul>
<p>Once your questionnaire is ready, your next challenge will be to pre-test it. However, before you look at how to pre-test your instruments, you will look at the other type of tool used in a community diagnosis survey, namely, focus group discussions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="focus-group-discussions-fgds">Focus Group Discussions (FGDs)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#focus-group-discussions-fgds" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a group discussion that gathers together people from similar backgrounds or experiences to discuss a specific topic of interest to the researcher. The group of participants are guided by a moderator (or group facilitator), who introduces topics for discussion and helps the group to participate in a lively and natural discussion amongst themselves.</p>
<p>A focus group is not a group interview where a moderator asks the group questions and participants individually provide answers. The focus group relies on group discussion and is especially successful where the participants are able to talk to each other about the topic of interest. This is important as it allows the participants the opportunity to disagree or agree with each other. It can provide insight into how a group thinks about an issue, about the range of opinions and ideas, and the inconsistencies and variation that exist in a particular community in terms of beliefs and their experiences and practices.</p>
<p>The discussion is usually 'focused' on a particular area of interest. It does not usually cover a large range of issues, but allows you to explore one or two topics in greater detail.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="focus-groups-in-a-community-diagnosis-survey">Focus Groups in a Community Diagnosis Survey<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#focus-groups-in-a-community-diagnosis-survey" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Focus groups can be used in the following ways:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="exploratory-studies">Exploratory Studies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exploratory-studies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Focus groups are a valuable method to explore a topic about which little is known, or little has been written in the past. For example, in order to set up a successful health education programme you need to understand people's traditional health beliefs. Focus groups can begin this process by providing the first in-depth descriptions of how the community sees the cause and treatment of certain illnesses. Focus groups can also be used to discover local terms used for signs and symptoms of illness, types of illness, and other concepts relating to health.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="testing-ideas-about-new-programmes">Testing Ideas about New Programmes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#testing-ideas-about-new-programmes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>In the planning phase of a new programme, it is possible to use focus group discussions to find out what the community feels about the new plan. You can use the method to see what the community identifies to be major problems or difficulties in existing programmes and incorporate their needs into the new programme. Focus groups can give you an understanding of how appropriate the new plan may be in terms of culture or technology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solving-specific-programme-problems">Solving Specific Programme Problems<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solving-specific-programme-problems" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sometimes programmes have been running for some time and do not appear to be having the expected impact. A focus group can be used to explore such issues and identify the problems that may be hindering the success of the programmes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="conducting-a-focus-group-discussion">Conducting a Focus Group Discussion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conducting-a-focus-group-discussion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In order to conduct a successful focus group discussion you should adopt the following four steps:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-one-preparation">Step One: Preparation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-one-preparation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>First you recruit participants. Focus groups are 'focused' because the participants usually share a common characteristic. This may be age, sex, educational background, religion or something directly related to the topic. This encourages the group to speak more freely about the subject without fear of being judged by others who are thought to be superior. For example, young women may not be as forthcoming in their ideas and opinions in the presence of their mothers or mothers-in-law. The participants should have prior knowledge so that they can come prepared.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-two-physical-arrangement">Step Two: Physical Arrangement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-two-physical-arrangement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is good to make sitting arrangements that allow participants to see each other. Circular seating is the best as everybody can see each other. Avoid the traditional classroom type of sitting. Ensure the room is well lit and ventilated and has minimum or no disturbance so that the participants can concentrate on the discussion. The environment should promote talking and sharing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-three-preparing-a-discussion-guide">Step Three: Preparing a Discussion Guide<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-three-preparing-a-discussion-guide" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should prepare a set of questions that will help you to guide the discussion. These questions should also allow free flow from one aspect of the topic to the next in a relevant fashion. This helps the participants to think logically and build on the topic you are investigating. Have a mixture of general and more specific questions. If your questions are all general, you may not elicit detailed responses from the participants. On the other hand, if your questions are all specific, you may neglect to address and receive information on the 'bigger picture.'</p>
<p>Make sure you have a variety of follow up 'probes' for each of your questions in the event that you need to clarify questions or have participants elaborate on their responses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="step-four-the-discussion">Step Four: The Discussion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#step-four-the-discussion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Before you proceed you should identify among your team one facilitator and one recorder and introduce them to the group.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="functions-of-the-facilitator">Functions of the Facilitator<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#functions-of-the-facilitator" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The functions of the facilitator are to stimulate and support the discussion by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Introducing the topic and all the other participants. Self introduction is better because it already sets in motion the tone of sharing for everybody.</li>
<li>Reassuring them and explaining the purpose of the discussion and the type of information required. The participant also needs to know how and where the information will be used.</li>
<li>Encouraging discussion by being enthusiastic, lively, humorous and showing interest in the group's ideas. The facilitator formulates and asks questions following the prepared guide. The facilitator should involve all the members but must remain neutral to all responses so that the participants can freely express their feelings, opinions and views.</li>
<li>Encouraging involvement of all the members of the group.</li>
</ul>
<p>To encourage involvement of all members of the group the facilitator should ask open-ended</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>questions. The facilitator should identify and manage individuals who dominate the discussion and deny others a chance to respond. One way of managing domineering members is to ignore them and instead give attention to the other members when they want to give an opinion. Maintain eye contact with the shy ones and prompt them to talk by calling them by name and posing questions directly to them. Build rapport and empathise as necessary. You should also watch their expressions, mannerism and non-verbal communication. Try to understand what they are saying, communicating or insinuating. If something is not clear ask for elaboration. Avoid being</p>
<p>an expert.</p>
<p>The members may ask for your opinion. Redirect the question back to them by asking for their opinion instead or what action they would take in respect of the question.</p>
<p>After the session is over, you could share with them the information they were asking for. Control the discussion without blocking their freedom of expression and keep within the time allocated for this. At the end of the session you should thank them for sharing their time and ideas with you.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-the-recorder-records">What the Recorder Records<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-the-recorder-records" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The recorder records all key issues raised in the session and other factors that may influence the interpretation of information in as much detail as possible. This involves noting down the responses from the group and observing and documenting any non-verbal messages that could indicate how a group is feeling about the topic under discussion.</p>
<p>The following must also be recorded:</p>
<ul>
<li>Date, time, place</li>
<li>Names of participants</li>
<li>Description of the group level of participation including any dominant participant</li>
<li>Details of opinions of participants as much as possible using their own words especially for key statements</li>
<li>Details of emotional aspects and the vocabulary used. This will be particularly useful for developing questionnaires or health learning materials. If possible, a tape recorder should be used as well</li>
</ul>
<p>The recorder may also help the moderator if necessary. She or he may point out questions that are not well explored; questions missed, or suggest areas that could be investigated. The</p>
<p>recorder should not be especially obvious to the group but should be able to communicate with the facilitator if required and help them resolve conflicting issues.</p>
<p>At the end of the discussion, the facilitator should sit with the recorder and review the discussion and complete the notes and evaluate how the discussion went. They should then prepare a full report of the discussion using the participant's own words. It is necessary to list the key statements, ideas and attitudes expressed during each topic. These statements are usually coded and written on the left-hand margin while the comments are written on the right-hand side. It may be necessary to formulate additional questions at this stage for those issues that were not yet clear or controversial.</p>
<p>Next, you will look at how you can pre-test your instruments in order to ensure that they are capable of collecting the data you need.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pre-testing-the-instruments">Pre-testing the Instruments<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pre-testing-the-instruments" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is very important to pre-test all the instruments you intend to use before they are finally administered. It enables the interviewing team to discern, alter or delete questions which are being misinterpreted or are too sensitive to be asked without offending people. It also gives you the opportunity to discover if the various parts of the questionnaire flow in a logical order.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="points-to-look-for-when-pre-testing-a-questionnaire">Points to Look for When Pre-testing a Questionnaire<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#points-to-look-for-when-pre-testing-a-questionnaire" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>According to Salant and Dillman (1994), any pre-test aims to answer the following questions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Does each question measure what it is intended to measure?</li>
<li>Do respondents understand all the words?</li>
<li>Are questions interpreted similarly by all respondents?</li>
<li>Does each closed ended question have an answer that applies to each respondent?</li>
<li>Does the questionnaire create a positive impression, one that motivates people to answer it?</li>
<li>Are the answers which respondents can choose from correct? Are some responses missing? Do some questions elicit uninterpretable answers?</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 168 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Does any part of the questionnaire suggest bias on the part of the researcher?</li>
<li>Is the questionnaire too long?</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="procedure-for-pre-testing">Procedure for Pre-testing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#procedure-for-pre-testing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>During pre-testing, you examine individual questions as well as the whole questionnaire critically by:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="asking-colleagues-to-review-the-questions-critically">Asking colleagues to review the questions critically<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#asking-colleagues-to-review-the-questions-critically" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This helps you to identify if the questions are clear and whether they meet the study objectives.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="pre-testing-the-questionnaire-on-people-who-are-very-similar-to-your-target-group">Pre-testing the questionnaire on people who are very similar to your target group<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pre-testing-the-questionnaire-on-people-who-are-very-similar-to-your-target-group" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It is also important to pre-test your instruments on a community that is very similar to the one in which the survey will be done.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="simulating-the-actual-data-collection-procedure">Simulating the actual data collection procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#simulating-the-actual-data-collection-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>If for instance you are going to administer a questionnaire, you should give each interviewer/interpreter a copy and ask them to administer it to the group. Each interviewer should pre-test at least one complete questionnaire.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="obtaining-feedback-about-the-form-and-content-of-the-questionnaire">Obtaining feedback about the form and content of the questionnaire<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#obtaining-feedback-about-the-form-and-content-of-the-questionnaire" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Were any questions misunderstood? Were the directions clear? Was the questionnaire too long or too difficult? How long did it take to fill it out? Was there enough space for the responses? You should leave in each questionnaire more space for answers than is planned for the final one. This gives the interviewer more space to fill in responses to questions which had not been anticipated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="checking-if-the-questions-produce-the-information-we-need">Checking if the questions produce the information we need<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#checking-if-the-questions-produce-the-information-we-need" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Does the question illicit the information that you need?</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="trying-out-your-tabulation-and-analysis-procedure">Trying out your tabulation and analysis procedure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#trying-out-your-tabulation-and-analysis-procedure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Does the questionnaire yield data that can be analysed in the way that is needed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="revising">Revising<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#revising" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Check the final draft by going over each question. Ask yourself what the information gathered from each question means and whether it will contribute to the study.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="section-4-execution-of-the-survey-data-analysis-and-presentation">SECTION 4: EXECUTION OF THE SURVEY, DATA ANALYSIS AND PRESENTATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-4-execution-of-the-survey-data-analysis-and-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Welcome to the fourth section of this unit on community diagnosis. In this section you will learn how to execute the survey and how to analyse and present the results.</p>
<p>Before you proceed, look at the objectives for this section.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the process of data collection</li>
<li>Explain how data is analysed</li>
<li>Describe the various methods of presenting data</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="execution-of-the-survey">Execution of the Survey<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#execution-of-the-survey" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This is another important step in the process of community diagnosis. It requires just as much care as the planning stage. It involves going out to the field to collect information from the sample population you have selected. There are three stages involved in data collection.</p>
<p>These are:</p>
<ol>
<li>Interviewing the respondents</li>
<li>Data collection</li>
<li>Data handling</li>
</ol>
<p>You will now look at these stages in turn.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="stage-one-interviewing-the-respondents">Stage One: Interviewing the Respondents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-one-interviewing-the-respondents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>By the time you get to this stage, you have already developed the survey instruments, trained the interviewers on how to use these instruments and even pre-tested them. You do this in order to ensure that the correct standards are adhered to during the actual survey. Once the real survey begins, you should continue to work closely with the interviewers.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Your presence reassures them and they also get the opportunity to clarify issues that may arise. On your part, you get assured that the interviewers are continuing to follow the techniques you taught them.</p>
<p>When the interviewers approach a respondent, they should:</p>
<ul>
<li>Introduce themselves by name</li>
<li>Show their identity cards for the activity</li>
<li>Show their letter of permission to carry out the exercise</li>
<li>Explain why they have come and the purpose of the survey</li>
<li>Establish rapport with the respondent/s so that they can feel at ease with each other</li>
<li>Ask if it is convenient to interview the person at that time</li>
<li>Should the person refuse to cooperate, the interviewers should do their best to persuade such a person to agree</li>
</ul>
<p>If it is convenient to interview the person at the time you have requested, give the client/family time to be comfortable and proceed. If not, ask for a more convenient time when this will be possible.</p>
<p>This must be within the prescribed period of the exercise as indicated in the permit. In which case the interviewer must return punctually as agreed upon with the family or individual.</p>
<p>If the person refuses to cooperate and remains adamant, the interviewers should politely thank the person for their time spent and proceed to the next interview. Sometimes people may refuse to respond to the question raised.</p>
<p><strong>Remember the rights of the individual. The respondent has the right to refuse to participate in an interview or experiment or medication.</strong></p>
<p>There are various reasons why people do not answer questions in a survey. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>If the people were not informed of the survey, its objectives and when it would be performed</li>
<li>If for some reason the person to be interviewed is temporarily away from home</li>
<li>Lack of interest in cooperating or active opposition to the survey</li>
<li>In order to reduce the incidence of such opposition from your sample group, you should always make sure that the community is well informed about the study. If the target respondent is temporarily away from home, the interviewer should make arrangements to return when he or she is in. People who lack interest need a clearer explanation and persuasion so that they can see how they will benefit from the exercise. Activists can be difficult; if they remain adamant just politely thank them and let them free. They are just exercising their rights. If you find that a large number of people in your group are non-respondents, you will need to do a random selection from that group and re-approach them.</li>
</ul>
<p>The results you get from the new sample will be representative of the entire non-respondent group. Compare the results with the original sample of all respondents and calculate the difference.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stage-two-data-collection">Stage Two: Data Collection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stage-two-data-collection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Interviewers should be advised to use a pencil when filling out forms so that it is easier to make corrections. They should not erase a wrong response. If a mistake is made, the incorrect response should be crossed out and the correct response marked above it. Incorrect responses should not be erased because it is possible that if the erasure is incomplete, the response might not be legible or might be confused with a different response. The interviewer should fill in the responses at the time they are given. No response should be filled in afterwards because the interviewer may remember the response incorrectly.</p>
<p>Ensure that every interviewer has all the tools they need to collect data such as tools for anthropometric measurements and laboratory specimen containers.</p>
<p>You should avail a convenient carrier for these tools and check the packs daily to ensure that any specimens collected were handled correctly. It is good to remind the interviewers to recheck their measurements before they leave the client to avoid mistakes. They should also check the forms before they leave the respondent in order to be sure that all relevant information is satisfactory filled in the appropriate space provided.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 170 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>If the survey involves the collection of laboratory specimens, then you should make arrangements for their safe storage before they are transported to the appropriate place for analysis. Laboratory specimens need special care especially when handling, storing and transporting them because one lapse could cause the loss of an entire day's specimens.</p>
<p>At the end of the day, all the forms should be checked thoroughly by someone other than the interviewer. If data is missing it may be necessary for the interviewer to return the next day to collect it. At this stage it may be possible to begin tallying the results so that when the analysis begins all you have to do is add up the tallies instead of going through all the forms since the first interview</p>
<p>The appropriate time for data collection was already covered in the sub section on how to plan a survey. Can you remember what you covered?</p>
<ul>
<li>When the sample population will be available</li>
<li>When the team will be available</li>
<li>During an appropriate season when people are not too busy planting</li>
<li>On days other than public holidays and weekends when people are less likely to be at home</li>
</ul>
<p>During data collection, it is very important to ensure that there is quality control so that you do not end up with false or misleading conclusions.</p>
<p>To ensure quality data you should:</p>
<ul>
<li>Avoid bias when designing the questionnaire as explained earlier</li>
<li>Provide an instruction sheet on how to ask certain questions and how to record answers</li>
<li>Select interviewers with care</li>
<li>Select and train the assistants carefully in all the procedures together with interviewers</li>
<li>Involve them in the pre-testing phase</li>
<li>Limit the number of interviews that interviewers can conduct in a day so that they do not become too exhausted</li>
<li>Identify assistants to carry out quality checks everyday</li>
</ul>
<p>This is the stage where you check data for completeness and organise it for analysis. The following guidelines will help you.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Check to confirm that all the forms have been completed satisfactorily</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ensure that questionnaires are numbered</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Identify one person to be responsible for storing data and specimens securely</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Record forms should be sequenced and stored with clear labels</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Make sure that all the information you need has been collected in a standard way</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Develop an insight into the possible ways of analysing data</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ensure availability of any resources needed for analysis, such as a computer</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Once you have collected the data, it is completely meaningless unless you can extract meaning through analysis.</p>
<p>You will now look at how to analyse the data.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-analysis">Data Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The data you obtain from the field is known as 'raw data'. In this state, it does not give much information and is therefore difficult to interpret. That is why it needs further work known as data analysis.</p>
<p>Data analysis is the separation and categorisation of numerical data into groups in order to understand its meaning. Statistical methods are used to do this because they:</p>
<ul>
<li>Summarise the data.</li>
<li>Make inferences about the data. This means that data which has been gathered on a sample can be used to indicate what is probably happening to the entire population so as to make judgement about them.</li>
</ul>
<p>The process of data analysis involves the following steps:</p>
<ul>
<li>Data cleaning</li>
<li>Sorting or tallying</li>
<li>Coding and entering data</li>
<li>Analysis of results</li>
</ul>
<p>These steps are covered in detail in module four unit three on Research in Nursing. You will now look briefly at them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-cleaning">Data Cleaning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-cleaning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Data cleaning was covered earlier in the subsection on data handling. Can you remember what was covered? In addition, you will also need to do the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Find 'missing data'. If one question is missing information in the majority of the questionnaires, then you can ignore it from the study.</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Correct mistakes committed by interviewers after confirming with them, for example, putting a tick against smoker instead of non-smoker.</li>
<li>Exclude all inconsistent information if you can not verify its correctness.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sorting-and-tallying-data">Sorting and Tallying Data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sorting-and-tallying-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Once you have collected data from the field, you need to organise it in a systematic manner that facilitates analysis. You do this by sorting and tallying the data.</p>
<p>Sorting is arranging raw data in groups or in a particular order. You should select a system of sorting which facilitates data analysis. For example, if you are collecting data on users of family planning, you may decide to sort your data into two groups, that is, users and non-users of family planning. If your questionnaire is made up of closed-ended questions, such as the yes and no type, then you can assign numbers to these questions. For example, 1 to Yes and 0 to No, and sort them accordingly. On the other hand, if your questionnaire has open-ended questions, then you need to categorise all the responses given and assign numbers to them.</p>
<p>Data which has been sorted or arranged into some order according to magnitude is called an array. The following tables show examples of raw data and arrayed data.</p>
<p>a table of raw data of ages in years of 25 patients seen in a survey.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>2</th><th>23</th><th>28</th><th>11</th><th>3</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>15</td><td>7</td><td>13</td><td>8</td><td>21</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>15</td><td>5</td><td>16</td><td>13</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>27</td><td>24</td><td>3</td><td>6</td><td>2</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>a table of an array of the same data arranged in ascending order.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>1</th><th>3</th><th>6</th><th>13</th><th>21</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>7</td><td>13</td><td>23</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>8</td><td>15</td><td>24</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>5</td><td>11</td><td>15</td><td>27</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>6</td><td>12</td><td>16</td><td>28</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Raw and arrayed data are ungrouped. To help you group data you use a tally sheet.</p>
<p>Tallying is one of the methods used to help you organise data before you analyse it. You will already familiar with the tally sheets you used in the outpatient or MCH/FP clinic.</p>
<p>Tallying is the setting up of classes or clusters which are tied by a slanting stroke. Usually four vertical strokes are made then a fifth stroke is drawn through them to represent the fifth item. Each cluster represents specific identifiable characteristics of the specified data. This data is then presented using a frequency distribution table.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="frequency-distribution-table-of-the-ages-of-patients-seen-at-health-centre-x">Frequency Distribution Table of the Ages of Patients seen at Health Centre X<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frequency-distribution-table-of-the-ages-of-patients-seen-at-health-centre-x" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age in years</th><th>Tally</th><th>Number of patients</th><th>Percentage</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>0-4</td><td>IIIIII</td><td>10</td><td>33</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>IIIIII</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>5-9</td><td>IIIIII II</td><td>7</td><td>23</td></tr><tr><td>10-14</td><td>IIII</td><td>4</td><td>14</td></tr><tr><td>15-19</td><td>III</td><td>3</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>20-24</td><td>III</td><td>3</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>25-29</td><td>III</td><td>3</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>Total</td><td></td><td>30</td><td>100</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the frequency table, you can see how the number of patients has been tallied in the second column.</p>
<p>You will now look at data coding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="coding-and-entering-data">Coding and Entering Data<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#coding-and-entering-data" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This involves the conversion of data into numerical codes which represent attributes or measurements of the variables. Coding eases the burden of calculation. Researchers recommend that the coding process should start with the preparation of a code book which describes in detail the codes assigned for each response category and item in the questionnaire. When coding data you should include as much information as you can. The use of computers has simplified the process of data coding and entry. Computers save time and increase the accuracy of results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="analysis-of-results">Analysis of Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#analysis-of-results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Once you sort and code your data you are now ready to analyse it. There are two types of analysis that are carried out on data.</p>
<p>These are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Qualitative analysis</li>
<li>Quantitative analysis</li>
</ul>
<p>Qualitative analysis is usually applied on data which can be counted but can not be measured,</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>such as, colour. It allows you to analyse the information in a systematic way in order to reach some useful conclusions and recommendations. Quantitative analysis on the other hand, is usually applied to data that can be given a numerical basis or can be measured, for example, age in years, weight in kilograms.</p>
<p>You will learn more about data analysis in module four unit three on research in nursing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="data-presentation">Data Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#data-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Once you have grouped your data, there are many ways of presenting it. You have already covered the frequency distribution table with a tally sheet. The other ways of presenting data are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Tabular presentation</li>
<li>Graphical presentation</li>
</ul>
<p>All these forms will be discussed in detail in module four unit three on research in nursing.</p>
<p>You will now briefly look at them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tabular-presentation">Tabular Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tabular-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This covers the various tables that are used to present data, for example, frequency distribution table and a contingency table. The presentation of data in a frequency table shows the classes and the frequency of each class.</p>
<p>Table of Weekly Dispensary Attendance</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age</th><th>Male</th><th>Female</th><th>Total</th><th>%</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>0-4</td><td>25</td><td>25</td><td>50</td><td>27</td></tr><tr><td>5-9</td><td>20</td><td>25</td><td>45</td><td>24.5</td></tr><tr><td>10-14</td><td>15</td><td>15</td><td>30</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><td>15-50</td><td>15</td><td>30</td><td>45</td><td>24.5</td></tr><tr><td>51+</td><td>5</td><td>10</td><td>15</td><td>8</td></tr><tr><td>All Ages</td><td>80</td><td>105</td><td>185</td><td>100%</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Another type of table is known as a contingency table. It shows how two variables of the individuals in a survey relate to each other.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="graphical-presentation">Graphical Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#graphical-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This type of presentation makes the data you have collected more easily understood at a glance. It emphasises any fluctuations which may be present and tries to make the material as attractive to look at as possible. Graphical presentations are also useful for purposes of forecasting the future magnitude of a series of figures given according to time.</p>
<p>There are various types of graphical presentations, these include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Histogram</li>
<li>Frequency polygon</li>
<li>Bar graph</li>
<li>Pie chart</li>
<li>Maps</li>
</ul>
<p>Graphs are a very familiar method of presenting information. They are more attractive to the eyes because even without looking at figures, you can easily see and appreciate the rise and fall in the figures presented and can tell when they are high or low. For example, the temperature chart that you maintain for inpatients is an example of a graphic presentation. Making graphs of monthly/weekly clinic attendees, epidemic diseases, hospital referrals or admissions is a useful practice that can help you to assess what you are doing and what needs to be done.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="on-a-notepad-write-down-what-conclusions-you-would-draw-if-the-line-on-a-graph">On a notepad write down what conclusions you would draw if the line on a graph:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#on-a-notepad-write-down-what-conclusions-you-would-draw-if-the-line-on-a-graph" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Goes up?</li>
<li>Goes down?</li>
<li>Remains the same?</li>
</ul>
<p>You should have included that a straight horizontal line on a graph indicates that there has been no change in the aspect being measured. When the line goes up, it indicates that there has been an increase for example, more people attending your outpatient services, when the line goes down, it means that the quantity is decreasing, for example fewer people attending your outpatient services.</p>
<p>According to Plews and Onyango (1987), the construction of a good graph requires the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clear, concise and unambiguous titles</li>
<li>Clear and concise statement of units in which the figures are measured</li>
<li>Correct vertical and horizontal scaling</li>
<li>Statement of units used on vertical and horizontal axis</li>
<li>A key/legend to explain the various features of a graph, if need be</li>
<li>Correct graphing according to the scales specified on the horizontal and vertical axes</li>
</ul>
<p>Some common types of graphs will now be described.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="histogram">Histogram<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#histogram" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 173 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>This type of graph uses vertical blocks to represent class frequencies in a frequency distribution. You show the classes on the horizontal axis and the frequencies of the classes on the vertical axis. While the horizontal axis need not start from zero, the vertical axis must always start with zero. It is used to illustrate any data where the variable concerned changes with time.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-dca5fea6.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="frequency-polygon">Frequency Polygon<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frequency-polygon" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A polygon is a many-sided figure. A frequency polygon is derived from a histogram by joining the midpoints of the tops of the rectangles of the histogram in straight lines. The resultant figure does not have vertical bars but is made up of straight lines joining the different points on the graph. For comparison purposes, you can even draw two frequency polygons on the same graph paper.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-9dc2a355.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bar-chart">Bar Chart<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bar-chart" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a graph which comprises a number of spaced rectangles whose length varies with the magnitude represented. The rectangles have the same width and may be vertical or horizontal. They are used to represent a large variety of statistical data, including data that can be represented in other ways. Bar charts can also be multiple, that is, representing two or more sets of comparable data.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-0bc40bda.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pie-chart">Pie Chart<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pie-chart" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You will have already come across pie charts in textbooks. It is basically a circle divided into sectors or pieces. Each piece of a pie chart represents a total percentage of a specific group or cluster. A pie chart is especially useful for dealing with data where actual numerical quantities are not so important. For example, when you want to find out what percentage of your population has access to water rather than the actual number. It is therefore used for comparisons based on percentages. For a pie chart to give a good visual impression, you should make sure that the total data is not sub divided into too many separate components. Ideally, the sectors should not exceed seven.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260117-28e16162.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="maps">Maps<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maps" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Maps are another effective way of presenting information. They are used to describe, for example, differences in the frequency of a disease in different areas.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="section-5-report-writing-dissemination-and-community-action">SECTION 5: REPORT WRITING, DISSEMINATION AND COMMUNITY ACTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#section-5-report-writing-dissemination-and-community-action" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Welcome to the final section in this unit on community diagnosis. In the last section you learnt how to execute a survey, analyse and present data.</p>
<p>Usually after all the struggle of collecting and analysing data is over, you tend to feel like your job is complete. However, an important and time consuming part still lies ahead. That is, giving feedback, report writing and community action.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="objectives">Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>By the end of this section you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the different types of feedback and their targets</li>
<li>Explain how to compile a preliminary, non-medical and medical report</li>
<li>Explain the types of community action needed to implement survey recommendations</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="feedback-and-report-writing">Feedback and Report Writing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#feedback-and-report-writing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Feedback means giving comments about how well or badly a person is doing in order to help them do better. It is a form of communication. You will recall that during the planning stage, it</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Page 175 of 267
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>was said that the community will only cooperate if they perceive some beneficial results from their cooperation. So after your survey is done, those in the community who cooperated with you are entitled to receive some form of feedback. They want to know what you found.</p>
<p>When planning feedback you need to consider to whom it shall be given and in what form. This will help you to deliver the message effectively and in a way that it is well understood by the people concerned.</p>
<p>All individuals in the community who are concerned with the health of the people are entitled to feedback. The feedback table below gives a summary of the different types of feedback, who should receive it and when.</p>
<p>Feedback Table</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Targets of feedback</th><th>Forms of feedback x= receives feedback</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>Individual results</td><td>Preliminary report</td><td>Non medical report</td><td>Medical report</td></tr><tr><td>Patients population</td><td>X</td><td>X</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Non medical leadership</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>Local level</li>
<li>National level |  | X | X
X |   |
|  Medical leadership</li>
<li>Local level</li>
<li>National level | X | X |  | X
X  |
|  Medical |  |  |  | X  |
|  profession
